### Post by the.phantom on 2007-02-09
well i used linspire for almost a year and gave up on CNR( after paying $30 for the right to use CNR)
and that is when i changed to UBUNTU and that was the smartest thing i ever did !

cnr had a lot of programs, but most out of date
example  firefox was 8 months behind before i upgraded it with some code someone had posted.when i went to cnr i would get ads for the av program they would sell me for $20/year

most of the programs were at least a year old or were buggy

and if a program didn't install it would hang the cnr and the joy of re installing cnr

i did make a few post when i was with linspire and the answers i got were folks trying to impress me with how much they knew about linux and no real help.

i upgraded to a better modem and Kppp would not work and never got a answer that would work, but got several replies telling me to upgrade to dsl , so i came here installed KPPP and did a search and one post and i was able to use it again 

i am very new with the linux and i have done some post here and have got good replies with good help


so for me this strikes panic in me, i finally have a nice stable system and have no desires for the CNR system. it did have several old 2d games that i don't play, a few non working backup programs that were outdated.
yes i would like a window media player and a few other things that i did get with the linspire system but i have found i really don't need them anymore so no thank you on the entire CNR system unless they can get someone to upgrade a program in a reasonable amount of time !

i had a friend download and burn me a UBUNTU live cd" and that afternoon i was installing it right on top of the linspre and CNR !

but that is just my ideas on it

### Post by GregDT on 2007-02-09
Well a final word I guess, I don't agree with the Linspire model. I don't believe what they do is good for Linux or good for Open Source Software. They are going in completely the wrong direction. To me they seem to be in the business of making as much money from Linux as they can by offering as little as possible. The world would be a finer place if they packed up shop and went away. That's just me, it's one opinion neither right nor wrong. It's just an opinion. Up to now I have been able to completely ignore Linspire. Nothing I do has any effect on it, doesn't support it or validate it in any way. My only link to Linspire is that I use a distro that is based on Debian and so is Linspire.

But now its seems Ubuntu and Linspire are much closer together. Ubuntu by association validates Linspire. They give them added legitimacy and support the continued existence of a business I don't think should exist (or at least operate in it's current form) any more than I think Microsoft should.

So I excercise my right to go find another distro that closer matches my own idea of how Linux should be. That's the wonder of Linux, we can all pick what we want to use. We have choice. And I'm exercising mine and we all have a right to do that.

### Post by Russty of Oz on 2007-02-09
Hi all! :) 

I was just wondering about this "marriage" between Ubuntu and Linspire and what it really means to us Ubuntu users?   Is this a sell-out?   

Just wondering if anyone knows all about it and where it is going to lead.

For those who don't know what I am talking about this is it: 

Linspire's New Partnership with Canonical and Ubuntu

by Kevin Carmony

February 8th, 2007

This week, Linspire and Canonical, the lead sponsor of the popular Ubuntu operating system, announced a technology partnership that will integrate core competencies from each company into the other's open source Linux offerings. This is very exciting news for both Linspire and Ubuntu users. Through this sharing of technology and working together, all users will benefit from quicker release cycles and expanded features, products and services.

Linspire is well known for the quality of the CNR technology, and Ubuntu is the most successful community Linux project to date. The marriage of these two efforts will greatly strengthen desktop Linux in its fight for broader adoption. Both of these distributions will improve significantly through this collaboration.

I'm sure many of you will have questions about this recent announcement. Therefore, in addition to the press release which I hope you'll take a minute to read here, I wanted to try to answer some of the additional questions many of you might have.

General FAQs About The Linspire/Canonical Partnership

What are the main points of this partnership?
There are two main components to this partnership: 1) Linspire will immediately transition from Debian to Ubuntu as the base for their Linspire and Freespire operating systems, and 2) Canonical will utilize Linspire's CNR technology for aspects of Ubuntu's software delivery system

How long have Linspire and Canonical been working on this partnership?
Linspire and Ubuntu first started casual discussions about ways to work together a few years back, but started more formal discussions on ways to work together over the past year.

Take a look at the Linux
OS building Chart

Why do Linspire and Canonical want to work together?
For the benefit of synergy - each can get farther, faster by working together than continuing to work individually. Each group does certain things well and there's also a fair amount of overlap in what both have been doing. The less time, energy and resources Canonical, Ubuntu, Linspire, Freespire, and others spend duplicating efforts on the common challenges that face all of desktop Linux, the faster desktop Linux as a whole can advance. The partnership also frees up resources for each to dedicate to unique improvements and innovation that are important to their specific users and market segments.

But aren't Canonical and Linspire competitors?
While both companies, and their respective projects, do compete at certain levels, they each have their own objectives and specialties. Both are interested in seeing greater desktop Linux adoption and growth. By joining forces they can work together to focus more attention on addressing common challenges Linux faces in a world dominated by Microsoft Windows, as well as having more resources to devote to their unique opportunities.

---

### Post by tageiru on 2007-02-09
This is a dissapointing move. CNR does not solve anything that the current package management stack in Ubuntu doesn't solve. It is just an ad supported cosmetic makeover that hides the real problem people face when moving to Linux, namely that ISV support sucks and that package management doesn't scale.

Let's solve the real problem instead of pretending that it doesn't exist by using useless abstractions.

### Post by miggl on 2007-02-09
Kevin Carmony (founder of Linspire and Freespire Linux) announced yesterday that they will be working closely with Canonical (Ubunutu's parent company). This cooperation will Linspire's bring CNR (Click-N-Run) technology to Ubuntu, while Linspire and Freespire will use Ubuntu as their Linux core (previously they used Debian).

Read up on the full article here: [http://www.linspire.com/linspire_letter_archives.php?id=40](http://www.linspire.com/linspire_letter_archives.php?id=40)
And DIGG it here: [http://www.digg.com/linux_unix/Linspire_and_Canonical_join_forces](http://www.digg.com/linux_unix/Linspire_and_Canonical_join_forces)
Join the Linspire discussion here: [http://forum.linspire.com/viewtopic.php?t=427930](http://forum.linspire.com/viewtopic.php?t=427930)
Join the Freespire discussion here: [http://forum.freespire.org/showthread.php?t=4626](http://forum.freespire.org/showthread.php?t=4626)

This is truly great news.

For those of you that haven't used CNR before and are skeptical as to why it is better than apt-get or Synaptic, give it a try. You can read up on some of the details at [http://www.cnr.com/](http://www.cnr.com/). In short: it provides users with one-click installation of applications from a user-friendly interface that is geared for non-technical users. This technology will not and is not intended to replace apt-get or Synaptic. And of course you can keep using your favorite method of installing applications. :-)

### Post by Adamant1988 on 2007-02-09
> **tageiru said:**
> This is a dissapointing move. CNR does not solve anything that the current package management stack in Ubuntu doesn't solve. It is just an ad supported cosmetic makeover that hides the real problem people face when moving to Linux, namely that ISV support sucks and that package management doesn't scale.

Let's solve the real problem instead of pretending that it doesn't exist by using useless abstractions.

I'm going to steal a bit of conventional wisdom from the World of Warcraft forums and say: 

l2notbeignorant

CNR is not ad-supported, in fact I've never seen an ad in it at all.   CNR provides an easy point-n-click interface that people are used to for downloading (and installing) packages quickly and easily, and even in bulk (using aisles).  It provides easy access to Commercial softwares (Star office for instance) and if it becomes widely used, it may get more ISVs interested in putting their wares on it.

---

### Post by tageiru on 2007-02-09
> **Adamant1988 said:**
> I'm going to steal a bit of conventional wisdom from the World of Warcraft forums and say: 

l2notbeignorant

CNR is not ad-supported, in fact I've never seen an ad in it at all.   CNR provides an easy point-n-click interface that people are used to for downloading (and installing) packages quickly and easily, and even in bulk (using aisles).  It provides easy access to Commercial softwares (Star office for instance) and if it becomes widely used, it may get more ISVs interested in putting their wares on it.
They have an ad right on their screenshot page: [http://www.cnr.com/screenshots.html](http://www.cnr.com/screenshots.html)

CNR has the same problem as most other package management solutions for Linux, they present a layer between the user and the ISV which is why most ISVs currently ignores packaging with the package management tools and instead just provide statically linked binaries.

Windows users expect to be able to upgrade their software when ISVs release it, not when it is added to a centralized repository.

---

### Post by compiledkernel on 2007-02-09
I think the real issue here, the issue in the past was stemmed from the monetary concept. 

Do you want to pay money to a service that will install free software for you? 

The original outsanding question is the clearest standing issue here. If you cant install Open-Office on your machine by finding it in Add/remove, Synaptic, or by typing apt-get install open-office-blahblahblah, then would you effectually pay money to a 3rd party like Linspire to install it for you? Is it worth paying money to someone to install what is already free.  CNR in its previous incarnation wasnt free -- You paid a subscription fee and that got you access to its warehouse of applications. You would have to pay additional monies for stuff like StarOffice or other "proprietary" applications as well. 

Now CNR is free and open. If CNR remains free and open, and only stands to utilize the current set of Ubuntu Repositories, without the use of additional repositories supplied by Linspire for the purchase and sale of applications, then I see little issue with this kind of move. If CNR goes to push its subscription fees onto the Ubuntu userbase, then I take issue with it, going back to the original argument that CNR is just a proprietary installer garnered outside of the use of free software. 

I support the use and proper training in use of Synaptic, Gnome-app-install, apt-get, aptitude, adept, or what have you, and intend to continue to do so over the use of something like CNR as a means to get software installed on a users system. Just like I support users opening up a text editor to open up and edit apache2.conf , rather than using some gtk-driven interface to configure apache with.

---

### Post by Adamant1988 on 2007-02-09
> **tageiru said:**

Windows users expect to be able to upgrade their software when ISVs release it, not when it is added to a centralized repository.

yeah, it's so Ad supported... they're asking you to upgrade to their gold service.  I wonder how Linspire handles paying itself for advertising... 

Windows users will adjust to the idea of getting their software through CNR since it's that much easier.  I imagine it will get to the point they don't even know a new version is out until it hits the CNR repos and they get the update.  The only solution I can think of that meets your needs is that every Linux distribution agree to an installer that all linux (or at least most) will use, say downloadable .imn (.installmenow, haha) files will automatically bring up this universal package manager which will keep track of the current dependencies that these programs use to prevent duplication of libraries and such.  If something like *that* were agreed upon, then I would most certainly use that over CNR, but for now CNR represents the best option available. 

> I think the real issue here, the issue in the past was stemmed from the monetary concept.

Do you want to pay money to a service that will install free software for you?

The original outsanding question is the clearest standing issue here. If you cant install Open-Office on your machine by finding it in Add/remove, Synaptic, or by typing apt-get install open-office-blahblahblah, then would you effectually pay money to a 3rd party like Linspire to install it for you? Is it worth paying money to someone to install what is already free. CNR in its previous incarnation wasnt free -- You paid a subscription fee and that got you access to its warehouse of applications. You would have to pay additional monies for stuff like StarOffice or other "proprietary" applications as well.

Now CNR is free and open. If CNR remains free and open, and only stands to utilize the current set of Ubuntu Repositories, without the use of additional repositories supplied by Linspire for the purchase and sale of applications, then I see little issue with this kind of move. If CNR goes to push its subscription fees onto the Ubuntu userbase, then I take issue with it, going back to the original argument that CNR is just a proprietary installer garnered outside of the use of free software.

I support the use and proper training in use of Synaptic, Gnome-app-install, apt-get, aptitude, adept, or what have you, and intend to continue to do so over the use of something like CNR as a means to get software installed on a users system. Just like I support users opening up a text editor to open up and edit apache2.conf , rather than using some gtk-driven interface to configure apache with.

I'm familiar with YaST, Synaptic, Apt-get, GAI, aptitude, adept, Klik, and what have you.  I prefer CNR hands down to all of those.  I installed mostly free software (there were some games I was looking at) and I almost payed for a subscription just for the aisles feature.  The only thing that stopped me was that my Freespire laptop took a 3-foot nose dive and the hard drive became useless.   

Their (Linspire) idea for CNR is to offer gold subscriptions, binary drivers, and so forth that a lot of the user base simply disagrees with, but others demand.  I know I would like LEGAL DVD playback on my machine, and CNR makes that possible, now, across multiple distributions with the click of a mouse.  I have absolutely no issues with paying for the service they provide.   

But, I do suppose it's been made painfully obvious that my opinions do not represent those of the entire Linux community, perhaps even not the majority.  I do believe the majority of linux end users now and to come would benefit greatly from CNR across multiple distributions, it's a potential solution to a lot of problems.

### Post by dvarsam on 2007-02-09
> **aysiu]Last year, Kevin Carmony (from Linspire) visited our forums and proposed the idea of porting CNR (Click'N Run) to Ubuntu.

There were two polls about the idea:
[Kevin's CNR Poll](http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=138616)
[Should Linspire Offer CNR for Ubuntu Users?](http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=138603)

For a while, it looked as if the idea were dead.
Then, recently, Linspire announced it was making CNR open source.
And even more recently Linspire announced it would start basing itself off Ubuntu while Ubuntu announced it would include CNR with Ubuntu:
[http://www.ubuntu.com/news/LinspirePartnership](http://www.ubuntu.com/news/LinspirePartnership)

The following thread is a discussion of this new announcement.[/QUOTE]

Very good thread here!
And a very good idea from Mr. Kevin Carmony!
I like it when all the Linux "Gurus" finally join up to make a stronger team!  said:**
> Why would Ubuntu users want to use CNR?

Several reasons...

1. Linspire supports dozens of proprietary applications, drivers and codecs that you may want to have access to. MP3, DVD, Real Audio, Windows Media, Quick Time, Java, Flash, nVidia, ATI, Bitstream fonts, and so on. For example, right out of the box, Linspire users have access to all these different file types. With CNR, you could, with one click, deliver all this capability to your Ubuntu computer.

2. You may simply want the one-click convenience of CNR. You'd get the software management tools, Aisles, update notification, CNB's, and so on.
...
...

I agree here!
But in your article you mentioned that a user will have to pay $20 annually...
I consider that a lot!
Based on the fact that Ubuntu users are much more compared to the users of Linspire, don't you think that the prices should lower down a bit...?
How about $20 for 3 years?
Please consider the fact that Windows Vista came out 5 years _after_ the Windows XP original release...
And at the same time, IF Windows XP Home costs $80 in my Country...
... IF I consider that its lifespan was 5 years...
... I would have to pay $100 for CNR to use it for 5 years...
So, isn't that considered more expensive compared to going out & buying Windows?
So, you need to reconsider here...
Unless you used different "charge" policies per Country...

Thanks.

P.S.> You would also need to create a "trial" version, cause I don't know whether CNR is worth subscribing to...
... I would need to install & tryout to judge whether it truly worths it or not...
Finally, it would be good if some of money involved, aimed towards the development of the whole community overall (i.e. improving programs, etc etc...).

There were two polls about the idea:
[Kevin's CNR Poll](http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=138616)
[Should Linspire Offer CNR for Ubuntu Users?](http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=138603)

For a while, it looked as if the idea were dead.

Then, recently, Linspire announced it was making CNR open source.
And even more recently Linspire announced it would start basing itself off Ubuntu while Ubuntu announced it would include CNR with Ubuntu:
[http://www.ubuntu.com/news/LinspirePartnership](http://www.ubuntu.com/news/LinspirePartnership)

The following thread is a discussion of this new announcement.

Whew!  What a busy last couple of days it's been.  Since the announcement on Thursday, I have wanted to come spend some time here. It's good to be back on the Ubuntu forums.  I was really impressed when I spent time here last.  

OK....I'm going to start reading!

Kevin Carmony

### Post by Kevin Carmony on 2007-02-10
> **raublekick said:**
> To be honest, I probably will never use CNR. But, it is important because it legitimizes Linux legally by putting a price on things that should cost money in certain parts of the world. 

Specifically to Ubuntu, it may hurt its image. To the Linux world in general, I think it will help with people outside of the opensource world.

I've spent the last two days with reporters, reading posts on the net about this, etc.  I certainly haven't seen the majority of reaction as being negative for Ubuntu at all, in fact, quite the opposite.  You can't do ANYTHING in the open source world that pleases everyone, so of course there will always be some negative comments about ANY announcement from Ubuntu, but I believe the majority of reaction has been quite positive.

This article from Steven J. Vaughan-Nichols agrees:  [http://www.desktoplinux.com/news/NS3891369349.html](http://www.desktoplinux.com/news/NS3891369349.html)

I believe anytime Linux projects or companies collaborate, it's a good thing for Linux.  The very nature of open source is that of sharing, and to work together when possible, rather than duplicate efforts.  Ubuntu will always have its personality, and Linspire/Freespire will always have theirs, but 80% of what we both do is the same.  If we can work together to solve those shared problems, such as better hardware support, more applications, etc., that's a very good thing for Linux.

I'm very excited to see a tighter collaboration with Ubuntu.

Kevin

### Post by Kevin Carmony on 2007-02-10
> **purdy hate machine said:**
> I can’t understand why Ubuntu is being targeted for CNR, if Linspire want to offer this product to the masses then make it available to all Disto’s and host it on their website as Opera do. Those who want in can download it and those who don’t can steer well clear. No more tears!

Linspire announced CNR support for several of the major distributions a few weeks ago, including Debian, Fedora, openSUSE, and Ubuntu.  ([http://www.linspire.com/lindows_news_pressreleases_archives.php?id=212](http://www.linspire.com/lindows_news_pressreleases_archives.php?id=212))

This recent announcement just confirms that Ubuntu will be the first to be supported, but the others will soon follow.

It's important that you understand how this will work.  Ubuntu users will have two options. One will be to use a customized CNR experience provided by Ubuntu, and the other will be to download a "plugin" from Linspire which will provide a "pure" CNR experience.  To what extent Ubuntu utilizes CNR technology or products from the CNR Warehouse directly in Ubuntu ***is entirely up to Ubuntu.  We (Linspire) has ZERO say in this. *** In other words, Linspire focuses on the CNR.com experience via the plugin, and Ubuntu is in charge of their own implementation of CNR in the way they wish, so as to keep it consistent with your plans for software management.  No one will be forcing CNR or any of the products in the CNR Warehouse on Ubuntu users, or any other distro for that matter.  

In fact, CNR is unlikely to be incorporated into Ubuntu by the Fiesty release, but only via the optional plugin.  This means Ubuntu has several months to decide just how much, or how little, of "CNR" to utilize inside of Ubuntu.  I think you all know Mark well enough to know he'd never let us, or any outside group control what happens inside of the Ubuntu distro.  This announcement just means 1) we're going to start working more closely with the Ubuntu community to solve shared problems, and that starts by us changing our baseline to Ubuntu, and 2) Linspire is there to help Ubuntu incorporate CNR in the way THEY see fit.  

I hope that helps explain things a bit.

Kevin

---

### Post by Kevin Carmony on 2007-02-10
> **drotter said:**
> Maybe someone can clear something up for me.  I'm not sure what this is supposed to imply.

Are there going to be software that is not in the repositories (that i can get with apt-get) that is in CNR (aside from codecs and things)?  Does this imply that things I could orginally get by doing apt-get won't be available anymore?

Or is this just a fancy "face" for apt...and if yes, how is it better then synaptic?

So far the only thing that i understand is that it will probally be updated faster then the repositories meaning that I can get updates to things like firefox and OO faster.  

I dont mean to hijack this thread...I just dont really understand enough to have an opinion.

Here are some of the differences between apt and CNR:

    * One-click graphical install and uninstall of thousands of programs.
    * Advanced dependency checking for higher install success rate (also available via command line)
    * Automatic update notification and one-click updating of OS components and software applications.
    * Program and services management.
    * One-click access to proprietary codecs, drivers and software.
    * Allows you to buy dozens of different commercial programs, such as DVD Player, StarOffice, games, accounting programs, Win4Lin, Crossover Office, Cedega, etc.
    * Menu entries and desktop icons (optionally) are automatically added when installing and removed when uninstalling.
    * "Charts" to easily find the most popular software programs by category.
    * Search feature to easily find software programs by name, category, keywords, similar programs, author, etc.
    * Clear, easy-to-understand product pages, including:
         o Screenshots of programs
         o User reviews of the programs
         o Description, versions, specs, file size, source, etc.
         o User support and help by program
         o Release Notes
         o Screenshots
    * "Aisles" to group applications together for one-click install of multiple applications.
    * Dedicated, high-speed server farms with huge bandwidth pipes for fast downloads.
    * Friendly naming shown alongside normal package name.
    * "Voting Booth" where users help prioritize new software to be added or updated.
    * Automated, on-line Publisher Program where anyone can submit software to be QA-ed and tested for inclusion in the CNR Warehouse.
    * Automatically retries and fixes any problem downloads.
    * CNR has a better than 98% success rate. (The < 2% failures are usually a result of modem dropoffs, in which case CNR will can automatically resume when connected, providing the user with a successful install.)
    * Many of the most popular applications are enhanced and improved before adding them to the CNR Warehouse.
    * Products and services which are found exclusively in the CNR Warehouse.
    * Tracks problem packages on the server automatically, 24/7, and immediately hides the product from CNR users and instantly notifies the maintainer when a problem occurs.
    * Easy, one-click access to a program's source code.
    * User profiles based upon machine for managing multiple machines per user.
    * Local or networked software repository for quick install of same programs on multiple computers on the same network or from a CD.

Using CNR.com and the plugin, you have access to all of the above.  However, as I mentioned above, it's up to Ubuntu which of these items they choose to utilize inside of their distribution.  Linspire has no say in that.

You can find screenshots of the new multi-distro CNR.com here:  [http://cnr.com/screenshots](http://cnr.com/screenshots)   

Kevin

---

### Post by Kevin Carmony on 2007-02-10
> **Jucato said:**
> Maybe coz Mark Shuttleworth and Kevin Carmony are friends?
Maybe coz Linspire wants Ubuntu to be a test dummy for offering CNR?
Who knows.
Will it hurt Ubuntu's image? Maybe for some, yes. Maybe for the others, no. You can't please anyone. Besides, it's not like it's going to be an official part of Ubuntu, right? Maybe it's just a hype. Again, who knows.

... but I must admit, I find the logo thing a bit too... enthusiastic... It almost feels like an Ubuntu-Linspire merger. It is a beautiful logo, though. Just without the "CNR" :D

Neither Canonical, Linspire, Mark, or myself had anything to do with a "CNR Ubuntu" logo.  In fact, Ubuntu won't even call it "CNR."  It's sort of like Firefox.  If you use pure Firefox, it's called Firefox, but if you change it, it's technically no longer "Firefox" so you have to call it something else.  CNR means the 100% pure CNR experience, using CNR.com and the CNR plugin.  Ubuntu will just use some "CNR technology" or CNR Warehouse product offerings in THEIR OWN distribution system, which will have its own name. 

Kevin

### Post by Kevin Carmony on 2007-02-10
> **matthinckley said:**
> if linspire were any good it would be more popular.. how much of linspire's profits go to developers... stuff

As you pointed out...

1. Linspire is very popular, but not in the same circles as Ubuntu.  Ubuntu attracts the distrowatch, techie crowd.  Linspire has never gone for that market.  Linspire has always gone for the pre-installed PC market.  Linspire has over 350 OEM's who sell it pre-installed.  

[http://www.linspire.com/featured_partner/featured_partner.php?sent=1&country=1](http://www.linspire.com/featured_partner/featured_partner.php?sent=1&country=1)

[http://partners.linspire.com/](http://partners.linspire.com/)

2. Linspire has invested over $35MM into desktop Linux, with every bit of code we distribute as open source, including key projects like Nvu, Lphoto, Lsongs, Mozilla, OpenOffice, KDE, etc.

Go here to see some of the places where Linspire has spent their millions:  [http://linspire.com/opensource](http://linspire.com/opensource)

### Post by Kevin Carmony on 2007-02-10
> **Enverex said:**
> Why would you possibly want Windows Media Player and Quicktime? That's just... mad. Having to pay to legally play DVDs you own is not something I or I guess most others would also not be willing to do, same applies to playing other formats. Cedega lie continually to get people to sign up to their product. ATi and nVidia graphics are free so I'm not sure why they are even listed.

Still yes?

Because non-geeks don't think like you and I.  The average computer user doesn't care about the open source movement (they don't even know about it), they just want a computer that works.  They want to plug in their iPod and have it work.  They want to pop in a DVD and have it play.  They want to view movie trailers on the web.  If Linux can only be used by people who don't care about iPods, DVD, movie trailers, etc., then it will remain a small, niche OS, at least on the desktop.

Linspire has spent the past five years trying to promote pre-installed Linux PCs all over the world to non-technical users.  Without support for the things they want it to do, they're simply not interested and will stick with Windows.

The key is choice.  Linspire believes USERS should decide what should go on their PC.  Not you.  Not me.  But them.  Holding back these options is as restrictive in their mind as any monopoly Microsoft has.

Kevin

---

### Post by Kevin Carmony on 2007-02-10
> **deanlinkous said:**
> btw - you might ask where the newest release of linspire is at too? How long has it been since 5.0 came out? Lets see.... Debian sarge was released 6/2005 and linspire 5.0 was released in 3/2005! OUCHY!!!

Keep in mind that Linspire is designed for OEM's.  Unlike 99% of you reading this right now, OEM's do NOT like a new OS every 6 months.  ***Linspire intentionally has very stable, S L O W release cycles. *** This is why Linspire has been successful with OEMs, because we do things THEY need.  Linspire may seem a little outdated, but OEMs tend to be very happy with it because it's incredibly stable, polished, "finished," certified on their hardware, etc.  Those things take time and can't be moving around every year.  Linspire 6.0 will be out in a few months, and even then, many our our OEMs will feel the pain of a new round of hardware certification, etc.  But it has to change, of course, to support new hardware, be compatible with the lastest software, etc., but it just can't change every year.  

This is one of the key reasons we came out with Freespire, to let Linspire have a path where we could be more aggressive in our releases and testing, which is also why we want to move Freespire to Ubuntu, to even further quicken that pace.  Freespire has 6 month release cycles, Linspire will continue with 18 to 24 month cycles, as required for OEMs.

Linspire has always suffered from not being understood by the traditional Linux community, because it was never designed for that community.  Geeks love bleeding edge, quick releases.  That's exactly the opposite of what OEMs want.  Geeks like lots of configuration options, complexity, etc., but this isn't what OEMs want to preinstall on their PCs.  Linux purists like experimenting with new UI, but OEMs want a UI that will be comfortable to the 95% of the world that has been running MS Windows for the last 20 years.  If you judge Linspire for what it was designed to do, from day one, which is be a Linux OS for OEMs and to be pre-installed (not downloaded and installed), you come away feeling very differently.  

Kevin

### Post by Kevin Carmony on 2007-02-10
> **daou said:**
> I would have strongly objected the Canonical/Linspire deal if I hadn't read [ESR's and Rob Landley's WD201]("http://catb.org/~esr/writings/world-domination/world-domination-201.html") a few weeks back.

I think they make a great argument for why GNU/Linux users need to be able to get non-free codecs legally and easily. This is what I believe CNR is trying to accomplish. I would not run CNR myself, and if it were forced on me, I would jump ship. However, like all things GNU/Linux, there is always the freedom to choose. This is another thing CNR is trying to bring to the table.

Stallman makes some good counter-arguments against WD201 ([here]("http://www.libervis.com/article/richard_stallman_on_world_domination_201")). 


Nevertheless, at the end of the day, it's about choice. How can I argue to someone that GNU/Linux will give them absolute freedom if they have to jump through hoops of fire, trying to avoid issues of legality?

ESR is on Freespire's Leadership board.  He joined because he agrees with us that it should be the user's choice, not anyone elses, what they run on their PC.  Pragmatic, versus dogmatic.  The nice thing about being pragmatic here, is you can be so without infringing on anyone's choice.

I may personally think Big Macs are evil, disgusting, unhealthy creations, but I wouldn't try to find ways to remove them from the market so no one could ever get at them.  As soon as we let others start making moral choices for us, you crack the door open to other freedoms eroding.  (For the record, I love Big Macs, but am smart enough to know if they are right for me or not and in what quantities. =)

It's very simple.  The CNR Publisher program ([http://partners.linspire.com/publisher_programs.php](http://partners.linspire.com/publisher_programs.php)) allows ANYONE to publish their software to the CNR Warehouse.  THEY, the developer/publisher, set the price, with 99% of what gets published being free software.  Linspire doesn't try to play "cop" on what WE think is good or bad for users to consume there, because we believe users are smart enough and free enough to make that decision for themselves.  The CNR Warehouse is designed to allow anyone to put software in, and allow anyone to get software out.  The choice is there for all...publishers, users, etc.

Kevin

### Post by Kevin Carmony on 2007-02-10
> **koenn said:**
> My thoughts exactly - I was just getting ready to post something along those lines. 

As I was reading 'World Domination 201" I was already thinking Ubuntu could be one of the distro's that could pull lots of users towards Linux. The CNR and the deal with Linspire to legally offer proprietary drivers/codecs/... for showstoppers such the current lack of support for certain media formats e.a. fits right in. 

I wouldn't be surprised if strategists/tacticians at Canonical are thinking along the exactly those lines.

The trick is, who gets to decide what is a "show stopper?"  Who will be that judge?  You see, those "show stoppers" will be different for everyone.  I'm sure for Richard Stallman, there are NO show stoppers.  He'd sit at home and bang two sticks together to communicate on the Internet if that's all he could do without being forced to use something closed sourced or proprietary.  On the other extreme you'll have the 10 year old kid who wants his iPod to work, wants to watch DVDs, wants to watch Quick Time movie trailers on Apple's web site, wants to watch different video formats off the net, wants to play the latest commercial game in souped up 3D graphics requiring the latest nVidia drivers, and wants to connect his laptop to the net with some proprietary wifi drivers.  And then you have the million various points in between these two extremes.  Who gets to draw that line?  Where does "show stopper" stop, and "frivolous proprietary software" begin?  I say you let the user draw that line.  (By the way, CNR.com has one-click filtering to hide commercial software if someone never wants to see those choices.)

Users should be able to decide what is a show stopper for them.  CNR puts any and all software out there, and lets the user decide which ones they want to use.

Kevin

### Post by koenn on 2007-02-10
I was referring to
> Golden emphasizes twice _that the real showstopper for Linux adoption _by forward-thinking end users is now multimedia capability &#8212; specifically MP3 playback, DVD viewing, AAC and WMA download, and other proprietary codecs. This is perfectly consistent with what the authors of this paper have been hearing from our non-geek friends for two years now, and it should be a wake-up call for our entire community.
(World Domination 201, esr e.a.)
and the general gist of (the second half of) WD201 that the workaround is to provide that multimedia capability - even if it means licensing, proprietary binaries, etc.

I posted that CNR could be an implementation of that workaround - yet you seem to argue against my post and especially the use of the word showstopper ?

(World Domination 201, esr e.a.): > 
The only way to get such a package of licenses is to have some entity put it together, and stand behind it with money and lawyers. This means a corporation (or arm of a corporate entity) which can sign the appropriate contracts and (alas) pay the appropriate licensing fees. For the sake of discussion, we'll call this entity "Streaming Penguin, Inc.", and their product "The Codex"
[ ...]
Linspire, as it turns out, is in a unique position. They are the only company with the legal right to ship Linux ports of Windows Media Format codecs, including QuickTime capability. They extracted this concession as part of the settlement of their successful trademark lawsuit against Microsoft.

In August 2006, as a result of having shown a draft of this paper to Kevin Carmony, we were directly involved in the planning for a **Linspire product with all the characteristics we have been describing.** Linspire wants to be "Streaming Penguin" in the hopeful scenario we described above. They even adopted our proposed name for the product: the Codex.

It's not only convenient for end-users, but also for OEM's who'd sell "Linux Systems" if only they could offer a "complete" system (without the multimedia codec hickups). Add that  to a popular distro that appears to be catering for Linux newbies / Windows-converts (Ubuntu), and you've covered most of esr's "What Linux needs to win".

> Comments
[COLOR="DarkOrange"]Sam's Anti-Ubuntu Screed
Written by Limulus on 2007-02-09 13:25:32[/COLOR]
The bias against Ubuntu in this article is just dripping; the author clearly does not understand why Ubuntu is so successful and so attributes it simply to "marketing". He also doesn't understand why Ubuntu does some things it does; the six-month release cycle is based on Gnome's six-month release cycle. And Dapper was delayed by over a month for the specific goal of trying to make sure it was of especially high quality, so he's factually wrong on the comment "every six months, ready or not, there is a release". 
 
Further, I take issue with his caracterization that "there are plenty of other distributions which are equally usable". There are actually very few distros (out of the HUNDREDS that exist; see distrowatch.com) that are "equally usable" as a desktop OS; e.g. the moment you are forced to do things via a command line for basic installation or regular tasks, you are no longer in Ubuntu's league. Even the old-timers at my local LUG, who could use any distro they want, are generally using Ubuntu these days. This notion that Ubuntu is somehow substandard because it includes proprietary drivers (note: in all cases they are freely redistributable) that actually get people's hardware working when alternates would not is fine for people who feel like potentially fighting their computers for hours to try to get them working, but for Ubuntu's target audience of mainly Windows emigrees, its a dealbreaker; they'll have enough of a learning curve to figure out the differences between the apps. 
 
Speaking of "free software", Debian's definition is significantly more stringent than most; no one can legitimately argue that Firefox isn't software libre, yet Debian decided to go their own way over trademark issues while Ubuntu is still using "Firefox". Debian is not all 'white hat' in relations with Ubuntu, e.g the t-shirts; see [http://www.netsplit.com/blog/articles/2006/09/02/having-left-debian](http://www.netsplit.com/blog/articles/2006/09/02/having-left-debian) in which a former Debian developer talks about how Ubuntu does in fact give back LOTS to Debian. And Ubuntu is even creating new things; one notable exmple is Upstart, the SysVInit replacement that made its debut in Edgy. In that same post, he describes how it would have been "a herculian effort" to get it started in Debian. 
 
Finally, Ubuntu will always remain free in cost and ship with only libre software and redistributable drivers; the Linspire announcement ([http://www.ubuntu.com/news/LinspirePartnership](http://www.ubuntu.com/news/LinspirePartnership)) is that (1) Ubuntu will form the base of future Freespire/Linspire releases (as Mepis and gNewSense are doing now; Linspire could easily have done this even if Ubuntu wasn't happy about it since Ubuntu is designed to be freely redistributable) and (2) Ubuntu users will be able to use CNR (see [http://cnr.com/](http://cnr.com/) and note that they're also doing that for other distros, including Fedora, OpenSUSE and even Debian! :) Linspire's business model is clearly shifting from selling proprietary software as part of a distro to selling proprietary software for use across the spectrum of distros. 
 If you are happy with Debian and Knoppix, that's great. But don't spread misinformation about others.

=======================================================================================

[COLOR="DarkOrange"]Response to Limulus' post
Written by samv on 2007-02-09 23:05:161.[/COLOR] 
The word bias is defined thus: "a particular tendency or inclination, esp. one that prevents unprejudiced consideration of a question; prejudice." See [http://dictionary.reference.com/browse/bias](http://dictionary.reference.com/browse/bias). My criticism of Ubuntu is based on a number of reasons - if you think I'm biased, then rebut those reasons. Else, all you are doing is constructing a straw man to deflect reasoned criticism. 
 
2. Not tying the Ubuntu release cycle to that of GNOME and not being aware that one Ubuntu release was delayed, are omissions on my part. Unreserved apologies. Not that it makes any difference to the thrust of my main arguments. 
 
3. There are at least five GNU/Linux distributions which are the equivalent of Ubuntu - I think that's plenty in a world where over 90 percent of desktop PC users run one of four or five versions of Windows. Your definition of the word "plenty" may differ. 
 
4. Debian's definition of free software comes from the same source as everyone else - the GPL. Trying to assert that it is more stringent is incorrect - Debian is not considered fully free because "Debian provides non-free software through its repositories and includes non-free kernel drivers." See [http://www.gnewsense.org/FAQ/FAQ](http://www.gnewsense.org/FAQ/FAQ) 
 
5. As to Upstart, you forgot to mention that the post you cite is dated September 23, 2006. One of the Debian release managers, Steve Langasek, has clearly stated why incorporating such a replacement for SysVinit at that time was not feasible: "As enthusiastic as I am about being able to replace sysvinit with something better, this is the wrong time in the release cycle to be making dependency changes to Essential, or even to the base system in general. I certainly 
don't expect to be willing to grant a freeze exception for a non-Essential sysvinit in etch." Steve's post is dated October 8, 2006 - [http://lists.debian.org/debian-devel/2006/10/msg00254.html](http://lists.debian.org/debian-devel/2006/10/msg00254.html) 
 
[SIZE="2"][COLOR="Blue"]6. Debian's problem with Firefox was because (and here I quote again) "the Mozilla Corporation has asked us to stop using the name "Firefox" in our version of Firefox, unless we use the fox on a globe logo. We can't use the logo because its copyright license is not free.[/COLOR][/SIZE] Even if somehow we could do this, they want to vet every patch we apply before we release a package called Firefox containing it." See [http://bugs.debian.org/cgi-bin/bugreport.cgi?bug=354622](http://bugs.debian.org/cgi-bin/bugreport.cgi?bug=354622) and [http://ze-dinosaur.livejournal.com/12083.html](http://ze-dinosaur.livejournal.com/12083.html) 
 
7. In your last paragraph, you make claims that Ubuntu will continue to supply free CDs - if you are an official spokesman, clarify. Else, don't make such promises.  
 
8. The gNEwSense website states clearly "We started this project to provide an Ubuntu based free software distribution without non-free binary blobs in the kernel." Just saying it is based on Ubuntu is only half the truth.

[http://www.itwire.com.au/content/view/9398/1090/1/2/](http://www.itwire.com.au/content/view/9398/1090/1/2/)

---

### Post by Kevin Carmony on 2007-02-10
> **Anthem said:**
> Kevin!  Cool!  Nice to have you here.  Welcome back to the forum.

Quick question that I haven't seen addressed... upstream improvements.  I personally don't use KDE these days, but when I did I remember thinking that Linspire's KDE was very nicely tweaked from the stock KDE from KDE.org.  I haven't used Kubuntu in a cycle or so, but a lot of those improvements would be very nice to have for Ubuntu.

Will Linspire/Freespire be sending their pathes upstream to Kubuntu?  Will you do bug management on Launchpad?

In short, besides CNR, will the Ubuntu community benefit from this partnership?

We have a policy to try and send ALL our patches upstream.  Obviously, the more of our changes that get put in upstream, the less work we have to do with each new release of a baseline project.  As I'm sure is the case with Ubuntu, some changes get accepted (in-line spell checking in Firefox, for example, came from Linspire) and some don't (our way of doing menus as an example).

But, everything we do is open sourced and we always try to get our patches adopted upstream.

Kevin

---

But, everything we do is open sourced and we always try to get our patches adopted.

Kevin

### Post by Kevin Carmony on 2007-02-10
> **neymac said:**
> CNR is a great idea. If it didn't exist should be created.
Ubuntu is not the only linux distro that will use CNR, others like Opensuse, Fedora and Debian will too.
If it was a bad thing I think they wouldn't, or are they all wrong?

My concern at the very moment on CNR is that as Linspire uses KDE the majority of the programs I saw at CNR are for KDE desktop.
I believe that after the join this will be solved and there will be more Gnome programs there.

It's true that Linspire and Freespire default to KDE for the desktop environment, BUT when it comes to applications, we always try to use the "best of breed," regardless.  For example, OpenOffice, GAIM, Firefox, etc. are all default applications in the *spires, but are not KDE applications. This is why we try to have ALL applications in the CNR Warehouse, G, K, or otherwise.  But yes, if it's in the Ubuntu repositories, it will certainly be in CNR.  

Kevin

---

### Post by Kevin Carmony on 2007-02-10
> **tbroderick said:**
> CNR choose those distro's. It's CNR that will support Suse, Fedora, and Debian. It remains to be seen if any of those distro's will officially support CNR. My guess would be no.

Then you'd be wrong.  Ubuntu is the first joint-public announcement, but it certainly won't be the last.  In fact, we're actually further along in our relationship with some of the other popular distros, but because they are RPM, there's a bit more technical work before we go public with a joint announcement.

Kevin

---

### Post by Kevin Carmony on 2007-02-10
> **jeffc313 said:**
> they why did it take you guys so long to open source CNR?

Some things take time.  We had to enhance it to be able to support commercial applications AND an open sourced client.  

Can't wait for Launchpad to be open sourced!  I'm sure that announcement will be soon, right?  ;-)

Kevin

### Post by Kevin Carmony on 2007-02-10
> **Vorian said:**
> LOL, gotta love RPM :)

It's certainly meant more work on our end, but out hope is to hide the layers of complexity from users who don't know what an RPM or DEB is.  For Linux to move to the mainstream, users need to just say, "I want XYZ software," and be able to get it.  CNR.com will be as easy to browse and find great Linux software as looking for a book is on Amazon.com, and then when you find what you want, click.  Done.

[http://cnr.com/screenshots](http://cnr.com/screenshots)

Kevin

### Post by Hex_Mandos on 2007-02-10

I don't live in the US and own a Windows license, so I don't think I'm doing anything illegal by using codecs and workarounds. I don't really need a new way to install software, synaptic/add-remove is all what a regular user needs. And I wouldn't want to see an unfiltered source for third party software. I don't see much benefit from this. ### Post by Kevin Carmony on 2007-02-10
> **Polygon said:**
> is cnr basically a different front end to apt? as in something installed with cnr will also appear in synaptic?

CNR has all the software you'll have via apt or synaptic, but then it also has additional commercial titles.  There are also a lot of features you get in helping you find, install, manage, keep updated, etc. software that you don't get with apt/syn.  

CNR isn't for everyone.  Apt isn't for everyone.  Synaptic isn't for everyone.  Ubuntu isn't for everyone.  Linspire isn't for everyone.  Windows isn't for everyone.  KDE isn't for everyone.  GNOME isn't for everyone.  etc.  CNR is just another option and choice that has appeal to many, but certainly not all.  

Kevin

### Post by deanlinkous on 2007-02-11

From Linux.com;
According to Carmony, Linspire isn't paying Canonical support or engineering fees for building Linspire and Freespire on Ubuntu. However, he says there will be revenue sharing when Ubuntu users buy software through the CNR Warehouse. "With CNR users can buy DVD players, CrossOver Office ... the margin in that [sale] we share with the vendor."

[http://distrocenter.linux.com/article.pl?sid=07/02/09/201256&tid=116](http://distrocenter.linux.com/article.pl?sid=07/02/09/201256&tid=116)

Ubuntu is not the vendor of the software. See if I can explain a little. CNR is sort of a "display case" for commercial software products. I would say that *spire is not really even a re-seller in the normal sense. They may be but I would say it is more of a consignment shop. The vendor places his product in the CNR warehouse for sale. Any sale that is made then *spire gets a cut since they provided the "store front" and the vendor gets their cut. The vendor being whoever created the product. So the vendor gets the CNR "store front" with the easy install mechanism and of course any users that are using CNR like proprietary software so you have already reached the target market. *spire gets a cut for providing that service. 

This also means when you have a problem you will have to play both "partners" to get the problem resolved. 

Now if Ubuntu ever creates its own CNR warehouse and gets vendor agreements then you will "shop" from ubuntu and it will be between ubuntu and the vendor.

Mr Carmony can clarify if I am off base.

(yuck, all that leaves a bad taste in my mouth) :D

### Post by kripkenstein on 2007-02-11
> **aysiu said:**
> Sounds a bit like that *Seinfeld* episode where Puddy wants Elaine to steal the neighbor's newspaper for him because she's "going to hell" anyway, and he doesn't want to steal because it's a "sin."

Heh, that was a good episode. Especially when the priest tells them they are both going to hell.

Regarding the issue of webapps, it's a tricky issue. Some thoughts:

1) One reason for the whole 'Software Freedom' thing was the freedom to tinker. That when you have a program on your computer, you can do whatever you want to it. With a webapp, you can't tinker because you don't have the software. So perhaps that type of freedom doesn't apply.

2) On a more philosophical note, who says there is even a program behind the webapp? Perhaps there is a human being on the other side that does everything and sends back a reply. If there was anything like that, would software freedom be relevant? (Of course, this is just a 'thought experiment', I don't mean it literally)

3) If you are against webapps, then you should be against all server-run software. Like Google and Launchpad, and I am sure most people here use at least one of those ;)

Having said that, I am still in favor of opening up webapps to be free software. But I am not as adamant about it as I am for software I run on my computer.

### Post by Kevin Carmony on 2007-02-11
> **Hex_Mandos said:**
> But wouldn't having two different ways to install software be confusing? Maybe those people who aren't interested in how their computer works (as long as it does) would never learn about apt or its frontends, but what about windows power users with no *nix experience? I'm not a programmer, and I just started using Linux a couple months ago, but I had considerable experience using Windows (despite hating it since 95). Only having one way to install software by default is easier to understand.

Of course, I see benefits to CNR. I just don't think it's what Ubuntu needs. Maybe Linspire users need that kind of way to distribute software. I wouldn't oppose others doing it. But I'd rather have an Ubuntu approach to Linux and a Linspire approach to Linux than two converging distros. Different targets, different distributions. Standards should take care of interoperability.

Users make that determination themselves.  

Would it be easier if the ONLY way to get digital music was via iTunes?  I don't think it's confusing that there are alternatives, because most users pick the method they like, and use that method, so to them, there is only one way.   Anytime people start talking about "one" anything, that usually comes with "kill innovation."  I like a system where multiple options are available, and users gravitate to the one that works best for them.  Some of these methods will die from obscurity, others will be adopted, and others will spring up with new and improved innovative ideas.  

If this dynamic weren't possible, then we should just all keep using Windows.  But in a free market, innovation has room to spring up amongst competition and multiple ideas.

Kevin

That's the opposite of freedom, when one group feels the need to dictate how everyone must behave.  I would never for one second try to convince someone TO use proprietary software.  I WILL however defend the right for people to choose that, if they wish.  

Kevin

### Post by az on 2007-02-11
Hello.  Welcome.

> **Kevin Carmony said:**
> Here are some of the differences between apt and CNR:

    * One-click graphical install and uninstall of thousands of programs.
    * Advanced dependency checking for higher install success rate (also available via command line)
    * Automatic update notification and one-click updating of OS components and software applications.
    * Program and services management.
    * One-click access to proprietary codecs, drivers and software.
    * Allows you to buy dozens of different commercial programs, such as DVD Player, StarOffice, games, accounting programs, Win4Lin, Crossover Office, Cedega, etc.
    * Menu entries and desktop icons (optionally) are automatically added when installing and removed when uninstalling.
    * "Charts" to easily find the most popular software programs by category.
    * Search feature to easily find software programs by name, category, keywords, similar programs, author, etc.
    * Clear, easy-to-understand product pages, including:
         o Screenshots of programs
         o User reviews of the programs
         o Description, versions, specs, file size, source, etc.
         o User support and help by program
         o Release Notes
         o Screenshots
    * "Aisles" to group applications together for one-click install of multiple applications.
    * Dedicated, high-speed server farms with huge bandwidth pipes for fast downloads.
    * Friendly naming shown alongside normal package name.
    * "Voting Booth" where users help prioritize new software to be added or updated.
    * Automated, on-line Publisher Program where anyone can submit software to be QA-ed and tested for inclusion in the CNR Warehouse.
    * Automatically retries and fixes any problem downloads.
    * CNR has a better than 98% success rate. (The < 2% failures are usually a result of modem dropoffs, in which case CNR will can automatically resume when connected, providing the user with a successful install.)
    * Many of the most popular applications are enhanced and improved before adding them to the CNR Warehouse.
    * Products and services which are found exclusively in the CNR Warehouse.
    * Tracks problem packages on the server automatically, 24/7, and immediately hides the product from CNR users and instantly notifies the maintainer when a problem occurs.
    * Easy, one-click access to a program's source code.
    * User profiles based upon machine for managing multiple machines per user.
    * Local or networked software repository for quick install of same programs on multiple computers on the same network or from a CD.

Using CNR.com and the plugin, you have access to all of the above.  However, as I mentioned above, it's up to Ubuntu which of these items they choose to utilize inside of their distribution.  Linspire has no say in that.

You can find screenshots of the new multi-distro CNR.com here:  [http://cnr.com/screenshots](http://cnr.com/screenshots)   

Kevin

May I suggest that a feature describing the ease by which the source code for free-libre-licenced applications is easily downloaded would be appreciated by some.  Does CNR do that?  And if it does. would pimping that necessarily turn the non-technical user off?  If it does not (provide the source), how do you go about getting the source, especially to the bits that were enhanced by the Linspire devs?


> **Kevin Carmony said:**
> Actually, the CNR service IS free, and even most of the codecs and drivers will be available free.  Commercial products, however, such as StarOffice, Crossover Office, etc. do cost money.

Paying the royalties costs money (I'd really be curious how much it actually costs you and how it all works...)  Are you saying that you are distributing those codecs for free, but without the licence needed to use them legally?  Or is the appropritate licence (paid royalty) included?  I.E: is this a way to legally use DVD decoding and some patented media formats?

### Post by Kevin Carmony on 2007-02-11
> **tbroderick said:**
> I'm asking _Mr. Carmony_ if Linspire will share with other distros like they plan on doing with Ubuntu, but I guess you know everything. All I know is you have absolutely know no idea if Linspire is going to share revenue from software sales through CNR with other distros.

Here is a list of everyone who shares in CNR revenue:

**1. Commercial software vendors.**

Anytime commercial software is placed in the CNR Warehouse via our Publisher program ([http://partners.linspire.com/publisher_programs.php](http://partners.linspire.com/publisher_programs.php)), the Vendor sets the price they want to charge for their software AND they even get to set the price they will charge us for that.  In other words, THEY determine both the list price AND our margin in that. 

**2. Free Open Source Software projects.**

These projects make money from CNR in two ways.  First, Linspire uses some of the proceeds from CNR to fund key open source projects.  ([http://linspire.com/opensource](http://linspire.com/opensource)).  Second, CNR.com has a "tip jar" where anyone is encouraged to make contributions to any of the FOSS projects available in the CNR Warehouse ([http://cnr.com/screenshots](http://cnr.com/screenshots)).

**3. The OEM or Hardware Vendor who pre-installs CNR on the PCs they sell. **

This acts as a very nice incentive for OEMs to pre-install Linux.  ([http://partners.linspire.com/builder_programs.php](http://partners.linspire.com/builder_programs.php))  Today, Linspire has hundreds of OEMs in this program, each receiving regular checks.

**4. Distribution**

As I've stated, distros, like Ubuntu, also share in the CNR revenue.  They can use this money to continue their work with their distributions.

**5. Linspire**

We are compensated as a means for us to continue to provide the CNR service.

So, as you can see, the CNR system is designed to fairly compensate all parties involved.  No one is FORCED to participate.  CNR is completely an opt-in system.  We can't force OEMs, other distros, or commercial vendors to participate.  They only participate if THEY feel it is something good for them.  Just like CNR isn't forced upon users.  Users can use it or not.

Again, freedom of choice.

Kevin

---

That's probably a great feature - if you need that kind of software. I don't oppose people using proprietary software (I do so myself), but I wouldn't want to have advertising for an online store selling proprietary apps installed in my OS by default. That's a lot like adware, whic.

The most common objection to Ubuntu for new users is that software like drivers, codecs, Java and Flash Player aren't installed by default. Most people consider those to provide indispensable functionality - a computer which won't play YouTube videos or mp3s is not a real computer, for many people. So I applaud the effort to add these basic pieces of software more easily. But I object having an installer for other proprietary apps by default. I don't want it, it's bloat, and contrary to the Free Software ideals I support. I have no issue if someone wants to install it in their system (after all, they're still freer than any Windows user) but I don't want to have to waste my time removing bloat from my system because a minority of Ubuntu users has special needs. After all, people who are so dependent on proprietary apps can choose another distro, like Linspire or Xandros, which would be better suited to their needs. There's no need to make Ubuntu more like Linspire to meet the needs of a minority, when that minority can just simply switch to Linspire - different distros for people with different needs.

For the record, I wouldn't oppose it if it was an option during installation.  I just don't want needless applications and adware in my computer.

---

### Post by Kevin Carmony on 2007-02-11
> **saulgoode said:**
> But you would be within your rights not to support MacDonalds and to recommend to your friends that they not either -- indeed, to do otherwise might very well be considered hypocritical. 

Absolutely.  Personally, however, I tune those people out when they cross the line of "suggesting" something to me because they are sincerely interested in my well being, and those who go commando and never stop going on and on about it to me, once I've politely explained to them I'm not interested in their views.  

I don't mind if a missionary comes knocking on my door now and then to spread their good word.  I actually find it noble of them for their dedication, and I appreciate their concern for my eternal salvation.  However, if after I have politely explained that I'm not interested, if they then proceed to dust their shoes off at my door, yell at me, and say that I'm going to burn in hell for not doing things their way, or try to pass laws making me do things their way, that's when I simply reach for the ignore button. 

I have found the Ubuntu forums to be very well balanced, respectful and open minded.

> **saulgoode said:**
> This is the thing I find most curious: Linspire's adoption of Ubuntu as its base. Ubuntu is derived from packages from Debian's Unstable repositories and committed to a brisk release schedule that could hardly be expected to have a positive impact on stability; also its relatively short history hasn't really provided enough time for a proven track record of reliability to have been established.  Given the concerns of your business model, Ubuntu would seem a very poor choice as a distribution upon which to base Linspire. Perhaps you could provide some explanation for this seeming contradiction?

Freespire will pull directly from Ubuntu and sync with your 6-month release cycles.  The Linspire OS, however, has a much slower release cycle, comes with expanded testing and hardware certification, and pulls from the stable Ubuntu repository, not necessarily the most current.

Kevin

### Post by Kevin Carmony on 2007-02-11
> **az said:**
> May I suggest that a feature describing the ease by which the source code for free-libre-licenced applications is easily downloaded would be appreciated by some.  Does CNR do that? As mentioned in the list of features, users do have one-click access to the source code for ALL FOSS software in the CNR Warehouse.  Anyone can find this on any of the product pages in the CNR Warehouse on the "Specifications" Tab.

---

### Post by Kevin Carmony on 2007-02-11
> **az said:**
> By this, I don't understand if you are distributing the codecs or are distributing the *licenced* codecs.  Paying the royalties costs money (I'd really be curious how much it actually costs you and how it all works...)  Are you saying that you are distributing those codecs for free, but without the licence needed to use them legally?  Or is the appropritate licence (paid royalty) included?  I.E: is this a way to legally use DVD decoding and some patented media formats?

It depends on the license.  If we have to pay per unit fees, then we have no choice but to charge for that to cover the costs.  However, some licenses are free or a flat fee, and those we can distribute without charging for.  For example, Adobe Acrobat Reader.  We don't have to pay to redistribute that, so we don't charge for it.  MP3 playback is a flat fee, which we cover the costs on and then can distribute at no charge.  StarOffice, however, has a large per unit fee, so we have to charge for that.

Kevin

---

### Post by Kevin Carmony on 2007-02-11
> **az said:**
> Absolutely not.  What I think sends a current of doubt in some parts of the community is the part where Ubuntu is supposed to always be free, with no "Premium" (non-free of cost) version.  Unless the seperation between the two entities (the Linspire company and the Ubuntu community) is well established and well understood, people are going to think this is the beginning of the end of Ubuntu being free.

I think a lot hinges on exactly what will materialise in Ubuntu regarding this.  Are we going to have three package frontends?  (Synaptic, Add-Remove and CNR technology?)  If not three, how will we (or who will) decide which one goes...

I can assure you Mark and the others at Canonical guard that belief very carefully.  This is why they want to make sure and control what bits and pieces of CNR go into Ubuntu.

As for how the incorporate things, how many systems they have, etc., that's entirely up to Ubuntu.  I have zero say in that.  The only experience *I* have any say in, is the plugin we provide for Ubuntu users to be able to use the new CNR.com fully.  There are no plans to include that in the default distro (even though it is 100% open source, etc. and technically could).  These are the same sort of issues you face with say your web browser.  Do you just include one?   Two?  Three?  Do you offer the proprietary version of Opera?  etc.  These are choices Ubuntu will make, not me or CNR.

I can say, however, that I have spent enough time with Mark and the Canonical leadership to know they take their commitments about Ubuntu remaining "free," VERY serious.

Kevin

### Post by deanlinkous on 2007-02-11
Maybe not replace....maybe be integrated into it....maybe be alongside it....

[http://www.cnr.com/faq.html](http://www.cnr.com/faq.html)
> 
**How will CNR be supported in Ubuntu?**

Beginning with Ubuntu 7.04, Ubuntu users will be able to use CNR to access commercial programs and proprietary drivers with one click of the mouse. In subsequent releases CNR technology will be integrated to provide an even better desktop user experience for getting commercial software.

**Will the CNR plugin ever be shipped by default in Ubuntu?**

Canonical plans to integrate aspects of the open source CNR technology into Ubuntu's software management system starting with Ubuntu's Feisty +1 release expected in October 2007.

### Post by deanlinkous on 2007-02-11
Well, there is two DIFFERENT types of CNR client. 

There is the software client you can download from CNR.com for your distro. This is for a supported distro where the distro itself does not want to provide the client either in the default install or the repo. That of course would be seperate. 

Then there is the type along the lines of the partnership with ubuntu. Where the distro itself provides the client software in some form or fashion. According to carmony, ubuntu will be integrating the CNR client in some form or fashion in future releases.

My guess is that either client is simply a fairly simple wrapper on top of apt.

In the past when CNR got "stuck" a user had to use apt-get -f install to try and fix it. So in the past one did affect the other.

---

### Post by bowhuntr on 2007-02-11
If you had used apt-get to install the same software that CNR got "stuck" on, apt would have frozen, too.

### Post by FyreBrand on 2007-02-11
> **deanlinkous said:**
> Well, there is two DIFFERENT types of CNR client. 

There is the software client you can download from CNR.com for your distro. This is for a supported distro where the distro itself does not want to provide the client either in the default install or the repo. That of course would be seperate. 

Then there is the type along the lines of the partnership with ubuntu. Where the distro itself provides the client software in some form or fashion. According to carmony, ubuntu will be integrating the CNR client in some form or fashion in future releases.

My guess is that either client is simply a fairly simple wrapper on top of apt.

In the past when CNR got "stuck" a user had to use apt-get -f install to try and fix it. So in the past one did affect the other.

> **bowhuntr said:**
> I believe it does, yes. When I had a system with CNR on it, I could install anything thru apt that I could thru CNR except the programs that I purchased, like Star Office.That's good to know.  For me one of the initial draws to Ubuntu was the power of apt and Debian packaging.  It's still one of my favorite features.  I'm looking forward to testing this out.

### Post by az on 2007-02-12
> **saulgoode said:**
> If a philosophy states that "we are working to ensure that every single piece of software you need is available under a licence that gives you those freedoms" then it might be reasonable to think that promoting software which takes away those freedoms is a "violation of the philosophy".

It's not black and white.  Many beleive that the one thing that will help GNU/Linux overcome these obstacles (either by making free formats more prevalent or by eliminating software patents or the need to obtain a non-free licence) is marketshare.

More people need to use GNU/linux.  It is is the users choice to install and use those non-free bits of software, then it should not be made difficult for the user to do it.

The reason I think CNR on Ubuntu is a good idea, is that it (seems like it will be) non-invasive.  It can fit seamlessly with a free-libre install and be ignored if that's what you want.  Users who want/need to use thoese bits of proprietary software can use them.  Those wou prefer to avoid them don't have them shoved down their throats.

> **deanlinkous said:**
> [LINSPIRE FILE FORMAT COMPATABILITY PAGE]("http://www.linspire.com/products_linspire_whatis.php?tab=compatibility")
Did you try?
Are you sure?

Then what would you think if I said using only software from MAIN (debian free software) I could play every single one of them? What would you say?

I have NO non-free software installed and yet I think I can play every one of those. Might of been one I could not get to work....maybe.

I think there is more than one that will not play.  But the point is that in a lot of countries, it is illegal to use thoese formats without paying the royalty.

So, if you wanted to set up Ubuntu in a library, for example, you could not make it read a DVD or play those media formats legally.

> **deanlinkous said:**
> [
Clean room reverse engineering IS legal isn't it?

Now you could argue about the mp3 patent (not sure there is really anything a end-user needs to patent so it is not illegal IMO for a end user) but did you use mp3 just so you could make yourself pay someone to get a "legally" licensed ability to use those?


The mp3 format is under patent and to use it in north america, you need to pay the royalty.  For an end-user, it's non-commercial use is free, but that means you have to install it by yourself - your free-software distributer cannot ship it to you without paying the royalty.  I am not certain about the other media formats, but I don't think mp3 is the only one like that.

### Post by The Grum on 2007-02-12
If this works correctly, this could be great. Heres what I imagine:

1) Ubuntu, Freespire, OpenSuSE, Fedora, Mandriva, etc, etc, etc, all have CNR integrated into the package manager. 

2) Because of this, CNR has the ability to sell software to most of the Linux using community. Commercial software vendors, who produce the software found only on Windows systems, suddenly have a single target market for non-Windows users. They dont have to provide an Ubuntu version, a SuSE version, a Fedora version, a Used-by-one-guy-in-Ohio-nix version, etc, etc, etc. They just have to provide a CNR version. Its up to the distro to work with CNR to make it install correctly.

3) New user can install Linux and get all the apps that were previously Windows only, without having to fight with WINE and can download codecs for his/her music without having to install a 3rd party downloader (Automatix, etc) to do so.

4) User wants to try GIMP instead of photoshop, as Photoshop costs too much. The user can download GIMP (assuming its not installed already) through the same interface they used to install Photoshop. Run for the hills, its a consistent installer!!!!!

5) User can get their applications regardless of whether they run Windows or Linux. The OS becomes irrelevant and the only (main) difference is cost. £300 (Vista Ultimate) or £0 (Ubuntu Ultimate 4x4 Turbo Edition).

6) Linux gains market share = gains influence = push for open standards / formats.

7) Microsoft gets a clue and starts playing nice. Duke Nukem Forever becomes available in CNR. (Ok, maybe not)


I disagree with the point made earlier that providing proprietary software will kill the incentive for a Free equivalent. CNR could recommend Free equivanents and I believe most people would at least give it a try. Why pay £50 for something when you can get something similar for £0?

---

### Post by Lunixfanboy on 2007-02-12

No, not at all. This CNR thingus is merely ANOTHER layer atop the native format used by distro-of-choice. The daily synch makes a mirror of the OFFICIAL repositories of all the distros, and this sits ATOP that, offering just a way to process the packages, So, nothing other than "gee-whiz, pointy-clicky" is gained.

Whether it's right or wrong, good or bad, in the States Windows Media, Real Media, MP3, Apple Media, and maybe some Adobe codecs are not freely redistributable.  Maybe for a home user who doesn't care and won't be audited it doesn't matter, but for a business or institution it's very important.  So no without ffmpeg and w32codecs I can't play about half of those formats.  The three most important formats for me at work are WMV (Windows Media), Quicktime, and RM (Real Media).   I have to legally be able to play those formats because the services we use and subscribe to use those.

### Post by deanlinkous on 2007-02-13
> I (at one point) thought that repos were a grand idea, and frankly putting software in a location where it can all be easily attained at once is another great idea. Create a repo and burn it to a disk. And as long as there is source and even dpkg then technically you aren't reliant on a repo. Just a repo is about the easiest so yes most users are reliant on them. But that is the thing about linux, it is a community and users help themselves as well as others. If you want someone to do it for you then you wait for them, if not then you do it for yourself and others if you are inclined.

> If the CNR technology were integrated into the actual OS so that we go to the ISVs site and click a link and have the program download and install (after all the appropriate warnings and EULAs and things) that would be fine by me, because I wouldn't have to wait for some packager to decide that I needed the package. Do you think adobe will ever want to be the responsible party that has to try and make it work on every distro? Do you think *spire would ever want to be this responsible party.

You have a point, I just do not see it as "doable" especially if we are talking about a somewhat "universal" installer.

### Post by deanlinkous on 2007-02-13
The framework that it would require would make them practically the same. There are differences that go beyond just a different package format.You would be asking distros to change what they have chosen to do differently. Not to mention a user can change a system to where it would not work also. There are major/critical pieces that vary between distros. 

Also dont forget - distros usually package the software not control it. So if the software writer wants to change something important then what is a distro going to do.

Sounds like you want Apple or some other OS that is self contained. Like you want a company/person that maintains all its own code and is in control of the whole thing. I want my choices. Not just my choice in package manager but my choice in init systems, shell, and other critical pieces. So either the framework would have to be so general that a ISV would still need to be a expert about the different distros or it is so rigid that you ask the different distros to become far too similar. Either way you cant win. :D
