You have incorrectly analyzed how I think.  Yes, I want things to "just work" but I'm not an idiot.  You can already begin to see the changes that will happen should the top distributions start to cooperate to make life easier for new users (this would include using or allowing very easy access to proprietary pieces, single installation methods, etc.).  The FOSS/free libre/"Don't make everything the same crowd will simply make something that suits their own needs, because that's what they like to do.

Should the top distributions all decide that CNR technology works great, and that it would be a wonderful standard installation method, there will still be 500 more distributions that don't use it and never will.   The wonderful part about Linux is that several groups can co-exist peacefully, until one group starts overstepping their bounds and trying to force their opinions on other people.   You'll not that never once have I said to dean "Free software is wrong, you're destroying our economic model with it!", nothing of the sort.

I believe everyone deserves a choice, and I do not force my beliefs on another person.  However, when I respond positively to an announcement like this, only to be told that "I'm helping to enslave the world" (paraphrased) then I get a little huffy.  Respect me, and you have my respect, it's that simple.   


________________________________________________________________

As for the matter at hand, again I'll just say it, this is 6 distributions or so out of a few hundred that CNR would be compatible with.  Ubuntu is a good choice, although I prefer that my OS doesn't try to tell me what morals I should be following, so I don't use Ubuntu.      But let's look at how little of an impact this will *actually* have on the people who don't want it.

Linspire using an Ubuntu base just means that Linspire is going to improve slightly... although I don't see them putting the professional polish on Linspire that you would get from Fedora or Novell, I can certainly see Linspire being a good bit better for it.   However, this actually has very little impact on Ubuntu, seeing as how ubuntu is going to continue developing as they normally would.

As for the CNR inclusion, you can't make everyone happy, but they're certainly trying.  The people who are crying the loudest are the ones who aren't willing to admit that they have a choice.   I find it a wonderful hypocrisy from the FOSS crowd. "We believe in having choice! Just make sure that you don't use anything proprietary or corporate".
This crowd needs to re-think their approach.    But as for the actual CNR, this takes pressure off of Canonical for the time-being, one of the biggest pulls is that proprietary bits will be there and easily installable with one click.  That saves a *lot* of time and headaches on any system that's going to be using CNR in the future, if you rely on drivers and codecs for your computing experience.  
____________________________________________________


> Sometimes, if there are extremists on one side you need to have extremists on the other side to balance the argument.

If someone like deanlinkous didn't post or express his opinions do you think that Steve Ballmer would just go away? He wouldn't. He'd grow more powerful.

Given the choice of zealotry or narrow-minded selfishness, give me zealotry anytime.

You have what you have because of passionate people. But as long as it works for YOU, you don't care how it came about. To me, that's narrow-minded selfishness.

Extremists of all kinds are bad.  People who are passionate about what they believe, and actually follow what they believe are not bad at all.   Extremists are willing to stomp all over their own beliefs in order to get you to think they're right.  Don't believe me? deanlinkous is a great example.  Go look at his posts  in one post he will say "I believe in having freedom of choice" and in the very next post will say "Proprietary software is evil and if you use it you're killing open source".   He is an extremist, and does more harm to his own personal "Free libre religion" than he does good.

People who are passionate about what they believe typically will use tact, and will think before they talk, or try to sway you.  Someone who is passionate about open source development would try to sell you on it's merits, they would try to convince you, but they wouldn't harass you.  I passionately believe that there needs to be a decent recommended hardware list/HCL available for the top distributions, to make it easier for people to switch to Linux.  I will tell you why I think it's a good idea, and I'll explain my side.  What I won't do is tell you your killing Linux because you don't agree with me.

As for me being "narrow minded and selfish" I believe in consumer choice, the people who use these computers need to have a choice of operating systems that doesn't severely inconvenience them.  Right now they have expensive and more expensive as options, which is (in part) why I think Windows is the dominant OS.   A single installation method does not only give the consumer the choice of Linux, but it would allow them to quickly and easily change between distributions in the future without any major headaches.  They could go from something like Linspire to openSUSE and not have to worry about a package manager.   I am not, however, saying this is a good idea for Linux as a whole.  Just the top distributions that have the most users would benefit from this.   

Linux is a big playground and multiple camps of people can exist in it happily, the new users will stay with the distributions that come pre-installed on systems (it will happen eventually), while the more advanced Linux users will continue to tinker with their systems as they always would.  A single installation method would not kill Linux.

### Post by saulgoode on 2007-02-15
> **Artificial Intelligence said:**
> Well you can install non-GPL stuff with and without the CNR, so make it difficult for those who wants the stuff. Those who don't want it can just ignore happely. I believe completly free choices for everyone even though might think they made a bad choice. I'm not liken the idea that some people are waving with a police club. It's up to the user to to make the decision. Just because (we don't know yet) that they may make it easy for those who wants it. I call it choices to choose which in my opinion is good.

I also do not like when people wave police clubs. It is one of the reasons I choose not to support Linspire -- it has an end user licensing agreement that restricts use of the software and threatens legal action if those terms are violated. I prefer the EULA of Free Software which places no restrictions on the end user and never threatens him (or her) with legal action.

Personally, I find nothing philosophically wrong with proprietary software being made available for Linux; I have even purchased a video editor made by MainConcept. As an end user, that is my choice. But I would consider it wrong if Ubuntu were to promote that video editor by including a demo version on an Ubuntu CD -- not because the program is "evil" but because people who have been contributing to Ubuntu have done so based on the understanding that they were working towards  the expressed goal of providing Free software, not providing enhanced profits for MainConcept AG.

MainConcept, Microsoft, Apple, Linspire, Adobe, NVidia et al. all have a right to exist and to market their products. I am not suggesting that Ubuntu should be trying to prevent this -- I am saying Ubuntu doesn't have to help them.

---

First off,  my involvement in this thread is not to incur that Linspire and the company that runs it is Evil. Its far far from the case. They are however about as fickle as a 15yo girl on her prom date in highschool. Change this, change that, change this , propose this, propose that. 

Adamant, I am merely trying to speculate against the idea that Linspire is a successful venture.  Linspire over the course of its life has gone from Lindows > Linspire => Freespire (based on debian, and created as a community project mimicking Fedora as with respect to Redhat) => Linspire / Freespire (based on ubuntu, continuing to mimick the fedora aspect). Ultimately memory serving correctly, Linspire won their lawsuit against MS for the use of the name Lindows...they changed it anyway to try to make it more attractive. CNR itself has been recoded and redeveloped a variety of times (Xandros has yet to do this to their membership warehouse, they offer new services, are debian based, and continue to be as successful or more successful in light of Linspire, and inspire a successful business model from all I can see, without bandwagon jumping). Of all the commerical Debian based Distros, Linspire is the one child of them all that for all I can see, has to constantly change in its attempt to be successful. My issue, my only issue, is that with this kind of adoption that the two communities be communicative enough to embrace the philosophies that both share. Sometimes those see it as hard to mix Free Software and Business Driven money making enterprise. I dont have an issue with that at all. Redhat has been doing it since the first release of Fedora Core 1, some number of years back. Carmony's job is to Make money. Admireable this is. I am not a stallman droid, yet I do believe in some aspects of the Fundamental bridge that is called the Free Software Foundation.  And yet other items I believe in involve making money. What I take stake with and issue with is the concept that Linspire's controlling company takes steps that seem just and fair and yet may themselves be no other than an attempt to MAKE MONEY.  The Koobox relationship (and its holding company Mirius) was suspect to me for a variety of such reasons. Honestly if Carmony himself were to come into this thread and tell me that his interests werent in the concept of making money utterly at all in any capacity, even remotely, Id call him a liar.   Just a simple statement that Carmony's job to his company, to his employees is to make money. Plain and simple. The same goes for redhat, for mandriva, for any other major corporate or retail driven distribution. 

What do I see here, just another attempt by Linspire as a company to do just what it was meant to do. Make Money. Im not saying its bad, Im not saying its good, and nor am I paranoid. Im just telling you to see it for what it is.

### Post by izanbardprince on 2007-02-24
With the Click 'n Run announcement, I'm cautiously optimistic.

The Linspire press release said that you can pay for codecs and "legal" DVD support.

All these things are currently available in the Ubuntu repository, but if Click 'n Run comes along, do you figure the official line will be "Cough it up?".

Has anyone used the Click 'n Run client?

I tried using Linspire, but the hardware support just wasn't there, and overall I found the distro to be severely gimped and (at that point), you had to pay for access to things like media players and other free software through Click 'n Run.

So a few requests...

Please keep Click 'n Run as an optional add-on.

Please keep the non-free codecs in the Ubuntu repository.

Please don't gimp other package managers and tell us to use CNR.

This whole thing just smells wrong. :evil:

### Post by Tomosaur on 2007-02-24
I too, second the wish to keep CNR as optional, and secondary to Synaptic / Aptitude. CNR would be an excellent tool to acquire commercial software if that is what the user wants, but it should absolutely not replace Synaptic, or have any impact on the direction of Ubuntu. Freedom of choice and of software are paramount to the success of Linux as a whole, and Ubuntu's position as a one of, if not *the*, most popular distributions, can have a large effect. CNR will be a great advantage, but it should not be seen as anything more than a tool to access software. It should not replace Synaptic.

### Post by Prognatus on 2007-02-25
Maybe this has already been discussed in the 800 posts here, but I'll risk a couple of questions anyway.

In the FAQ on [www.cnr.com](www.cnr.com) it says among other things:

**Linspire and Freespire fully incorporate CNR throughout. (Ubuntu will be incorporating aspects of CNR technology in the future.)**

...and:

**Canonical plans to integrate aspects of the open source CNR technology into Ubuntu's software management system starting with Ubuntu's Feisty +1 release expected in October 2007.**

Q: How do these "aspects" differ from the full CNR solution and why can't Ubuntu implement the full version?

Q: What aspects of CNR will be implemented in Feisty+1? (What is not going in Feisty, but left for next time?)

Q: When a user installs (upgrades) Ubuntu, will CNR then record in its central database all existing apps on the PC too, so that the user can recover later to a full system with the CNR recovery utility?

Q: Features in Feisty are now frozen. So is CNR going to be ready for Feisty? If so, why haven't any of [the Herd releases](http://www.ubuntu.com/testing) mentioned it? And why aren't there any references to CNR in [the feature list](https://blueprints.launchpad.net/ubuntu/feisty/+specs)? Maybe I'm getting blind, but I can't see any...

There is a real opportunity for CNR to make a positive impact in a business environment where licensing is really important.  Until the poor perception of Linspire is changed CNR and other technologies or platforms it promotes will be viewed with suspicion.  There are definitely more than a few people wondering what Linspire has up it's sleeve.

### Post by Jim Taylor on 2007-03-26
> **Vorian said:**
> I still think its a Linspire marketing scheme.

I can't honestly tell you that it isn't, because it's very possible that this is one of Kevin Carmony's many motivations for this project.  I can honestly tell you that I don't *believe* it's a Linspire marketing scheme.  Why not?  Well, if I were trying to get more market-share for Linspire, I wouldn't give away CNR, not to Ubuntu or anyone else.  Until recently, CNR was the single strongest selling-point for Linspire.  Now it's available to any Linux distro that wants it.  If that's a marketing ploy, it looks to me to be a really lame one.  Don't get me wrong--I'm very happy that Linspire is doing this, and equally happy that Ubuntu will be the first one to get it.

> **Vorian said:**
> How much does it cost to be a lifetime Linspire insider Jim (just curious)?

So far as I know, it's no longer available at any price.  

Back several years ago, when I gathered every penny I had or could borrow on short notice and sent it to then-CEO of Lindows, Inc., Michael Robertson, to help pay the company's legal fees when Microsoft was trying to bury the company under lawsuits in the U.S. and a dozen other countries, I *thought* that all I was going to get was a T-shirt with a "Free Choice" logo on it--and possibly, hopefully, the survival of a company I very much wanted to survive.

Who'd have thought a corporation could be grateful?  When the company *did* survive, all of us who had helped keep it from going under were given Lifetime Insider status, together with a lifetime CNR Gold subscription.

I think Mike Robertson figured we'd given him something no amount of money could have bought, so he figured out how to return the favor, and did.

Regards,

---

### Post by FyreBrand on 2007-03-26
Jim, it isn't baloney.  Linspire has a poor reputation.  It isn't like Fedora or SLED that people like or don't, but realize they are solid platforms.  Linspire just doesn't have that underneath of it.  I'm not a huge fan of RPM package management.  Even though that is what we use at work (RHEL and SLED) I realize they are solid platforms that are grounded in what they are and have a discernible direction for the future.  It's hard to say that about Linspire.

By foundation I meant corporation, organization, group, etc.  The group of people running that business.  I suppose I should have chosen better wording.

Sure we can add "by me and people like me"  Do you know who those people are?  They are the IT department employees and managers.  When looking at platform solutions we're always looking at ways to reduce our Microsoft licensing dependency.  Linspire TCO doesn't do much to reduce that.  For a business with an IT department it's just not a considerable solution.

Jim those are honest criticisms and not because "I don't like your distro".  We aren't Microsoft employees but we (at work) do wonder what angle Linspire is going at to try and make more money.  Just because you don't like the criticism doesn't mean you should make ad hominem accusations.
